Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c04366/asset/images/medium/nn0c04366_0007.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c04366/asset/images/large/nn0c04366_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.0c04366&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Sodium-ion batteries have attracted widespread attention for cost-effective and large-scale electric energy storage. However, their practical deployment has been largely retarded by the lack of choice of efficient anode materials featuring large capacity and electrochemical stability and robustness. Herein, we report a durian-inspired design and template-free fabrication of a robust sodium anode based on triangular pyramid arrays of Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> alloy electrodeposited on Cu substrates. The Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> arrays exhibit an appreciable electrochemical robustness for sodium storage, sustaining a reversible capability 335 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at a high rate of 2.5 A g<sup>–1</sup> and 87% of the initial capacity over 2000 cycles. We further demonstrate the applicability of the Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> array anode in sodium full cells by pairing it with a Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C cathode. This full cell achieves a high specific energy of 203 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup> (based on both active electrodes). Such an enhanced performance is attributed to the thorny-durian-like architecture and bimetallic alloy composition. The pyramid tip induces ion enrichment for rapid charge-transfer reaction, while the alloy design reduces the electrode volume swelling for stable Na cycling.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D9117%26pageCount%3D8%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DJiangfeng%2BNi%252C%2BXinyan%2BLi%252C%2BMenglei%2BSun%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D14%26issueNum%3D7%26contentID%3Dacsnano.0c04366%26title%3DDurian-Inspired%2BDesign%2Bof%2BBismuth%25E2%2580%2593Antimony%2BAlloy%2BArrays%2Bfor%2BRobust%2BSodium%2BStorage%26numPages%3D8%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1936-0851%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dancac3%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D9124%26publicationDate%3DJuly%2B2020" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2020 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291021&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Alloys" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Alloys" class="article__tags__link">Alloys</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291952&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrochemical cells" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrochemical%20cells" class="article__tags__link">Electrochemical cells</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290667&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Ions" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Ions" class="article__tags__link">Ions</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291251&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Sodium" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Sodium" class="article__tags__link">Sodium</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Battery&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20battery">sodium-ion battery</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anode">anode</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Bismuth&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_bismuth">bismuth</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Antimony&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_antimony">antimony</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=3D+Arrays&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_3D%20arrays">3D arrays</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="NLM_p first">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are emerging as a gifted battery technology for the efficient storage of renewables owing to the abundant resource and low cost of sodium. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 1 to 4">(1−4)</a> At present, one of the key challenges to the development of efficient sodium technology is to find proper sodium anode materials. While carbonaceous materials and some transition metal oxides have interstitial sites to accommodate Na<sup>+</sup> ions, their reversible capacity is usually limited to 200–300 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5 ref6 ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6 ref7" aria-label="reference 5 to 7">(5−7)</a> To build a high-performance SIB comparable to a lithium-ion counterpart, it is imperative to develop anode materials with higher capacity. To this end, alloy-type anodes such as tin (Sn), antimony (Sb), and bismuth (Bi) have been extensively investigated because of their large theoretical capacity and highly reversible alloying/dealloying reaction. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8 ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 8 and 9">(8,9)</a> Among these alloying anodes for sodium, Sb is definitely an appealing option owing to its high theoretical capacity of 660 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and large packing density. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10" aria-label="reference 10">(10)</a> More importantly, its operating potential of ∼0.5 V (<i>vs</i> Na<sup>+</sup>/Na, unless otherwise designated) lies just below the lowest unoccupied molecular orbital of the ester electrolytes, which avoids the triggering of many side reactions while not compromising the working voltage of full cells. However, the large capacity also renders a significant volume swelling upon the uptake of Na ions (390% for Sb → Na<sub>3</sub>Sb), leading to possible electrode crack and pulverization and thus rapid capacity decay upon Na cycling. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11 ref12 ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 11 to 13">(11−13)</a> Although Darwiche <i>et</i><i>al</i>. reported a surprising stability of an Sb anode over 160 cycles, the long-term cyclability of Sb remains unclear. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10" aria-label="reference 10">(10)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">To improve the structural and cyclic stability of Sb anode materials, several engineering strategies such as reducing particle size, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14 ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref15" aria-label="reference 14 and 15">(14,15)</a> design of hollow structure, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16 ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 16 and 17">(16,17)</a> and incorporation of a carbon matrix <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18 ref19 ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 18 to 20">(18−20)</a> have been proposed and proven vastly effective. These designs allow the accommodation of Na<sup>+</sup>-driven stress and strain to some extent, thus resulting in a better electrode stability. However, Sb nanoparticles tend to grow large upon electrochemical cycling, whereas the introduction of carbon reduces the Coulombic efficiency of Sb anodes significantly. Another appropriate solution to this issue refers to the construction of three-dimensional (3D) architecture electrodes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> The electrode architecting effectively regulates the Na<sup>+</sup> uptake and release process to maintain the electrode integrity. Recently, our group has taken advantage of architecture engineering to design a series of efficient anodes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 22 to 28">(22−28)</a> However, capacity fading is still evident for 3D Sb architecture upon extended cycling, as the volume swelling might exceed the tolerance degree of electrodes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> To reduce the electrode swelling, a bimetallic Bi–Sb alloy would be a better choice than Sb metal. This is because Bi exhibits a similar electrochemical behavior but much smaller Na<sup>+</sup>-driven swelling (250% for Bi → Na<sub>3</sub>Bi) compared to Sb. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30 ref31 ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31 ref32" aria-label="reference 30 to 32">(30−32)</a> More importantly, Bi and Sb can form alloys at any molar ratio, allowing the flexible tuning of composition for high electrochemical performance. Although the design of Bi–Sb alloy anodes has been reported, their architecture electrodes have not been explored to date. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33 ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 33 and 34">(33,34)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">Bioinspired designs of functional materials have been an important research area in recent years. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> The resulting materials often display combinations of structural and electrochemical properties outperforming conventional counterparts. Herein, we demonstrate a durian-inspired materials design of a robust sodium anode based on triangular pyramid arrays of Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> alloy. The pyramid arrays are straightly electrodeposited on a Cu substrate <i>via</i> a template-free route from a glycol electrolyte of BiCl<sub>3</sub> and SbCl<sub>3</sub>. The ordered architecture of Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub>, mimicking the shape of a tropical fruit, durian, shows several key virtues for electrochemical sodium storage. (1) This pyramid shape exposes a large electrode surface to the electrolyte, maximizing the electrochemical reaction area. (2) The bimetallic composition offers an opportunity for an optimal trade-off between the electrode capability and stability. (3) The triangle pyramids help establish a concentration field around their sharp tips by enriching Na<sup>+</sup> ions, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36 ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36 ref37" aria-label="reference 36 and 37">(36,37)</a> resulting in drastic acceleration of ion transportation and electrode reaction. As a result, the Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> array electrode exhibits a stable and robust sodium storage performance, affording an appreciable capacity retention of 87% over 2000 cycles at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup> and a capability of 335 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at a high rate of 2.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>. In addition, the full cell consisting of a Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> anode and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C cathode is capable of delivering a specific energy of 203 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup> (based on both active materials), demonstrating a distinct potential in practical applications.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The alloy array electrodes were prepared by a two-step process, <i>i</i>.<i>e</i>., template-free electrodeposition of pyramid arrays on Cu substrates and subsequent thermal annealing at a temperature of 200 °C in N<sub>2</sub> flow (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) were used to characterize the deposited products. The galvanostatic electrodeposition is performed in a glycol electrolyte of 0.04 M BiCl<sub>3</sub> and 0.05 M SbCl<sub>3</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c04366/suppl_file/nn0c04366_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>). As-deposited products exhibit a durian-like shape, with numerous triangular pyramids forming an organized arrangement (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c04366/suppl_file/nn0c04366_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>). As the deposition is free of template, the pyramid shape would be closely linked with the intricate interplay between the release and depletion of metal ions. This interplay is due to the formation of complex metal ions containing ethylenediamine ligands (C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>8</sub>N<sub>2</sub>···M, M = Bi<sup>3+</sup> or Sb<sup>3+</sup>) in solution. The release and depletion of metal ions will determine the solution supersaturation, which tailors the crystallization process. Also, the self-organization of pyramids would be regulated by the additives of boric acid and ethylenediamine hydrochloric acid in the electrolyte. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38 ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38 ref39" aria-label="reference 38 and 39">(38,39)</a> The ethylenediamine hydrochloric acid acts as a chelating agent to control the release of metal ions and the solution supersaturation. A low level of supersaturation results in low rates of nucleation and crystal growth, which naturally favor the formation of large crystals. In addition, the boric acid as a buffering agent keeps the pH value of solution within certain boundaries, which is desirable for the continuous growth of large pyramid crystals. In control experiments without these additives, as-deposited products show random shapes and arrangements (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c04366/suppl_file/nn0c04366_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>). No appreciable morphology change occurs when as-deposited products are annealed at 200 °C (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b). The composition of Bi–Sb alloy is determined to be Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> by inductively coupled plasma atomic emission spectrometry. The energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) reveals a homogeneous elemental distribution of Bi and Sb throughout the sample, indicating the generation of a solid solution (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c). TEM images in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d and e further confirm the generation of alloy pyramids. The lattice fringes with an interlayer spacing of 0.318 nm correspond to the (012) plane of the hexagonal phase of Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub>. This structure is further corroborated by selected-area electron diffraction shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>f.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c04366/asset/images/medium/nn0c04366_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c04366/asset/images/large/nn0c04366_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Synthesis and morphological characterization of durian-like Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> pyramid arrays. (a) Schematic of the synthesis process. (b) SEM image of Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> pyramids. Inset is an enlarged image. (c) Element mappings of Bi and Sb of Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> by EDS. (d) TEM image of a single pyramid showing the tip. (e) High-resolution TEM image showing the <i>d</i>-spacing of 0.318 nm correlating to the (102) plane of the hexagonal phase. (f) Selected-area electron diffraction.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c04366/asset/images/large/nn0c04366_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.0c04366&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">X-ray diffraction (XRD) reveals the hexagonal structure of Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> annealed at 200 °C with a space group of <i>R</i>3̅<i>m</i> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a). The strongest (012) diffraction peaks of Bi and Sb are located at 27.1° and 28.7°, respectively, while it shifts to 27.4° for the Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> phase. This peak shift is an indication of the formation of an alloy, because Bi and Sb share the same crystalline structure (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c04366/suppl_file/nn0c04366_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>). Note that the diffraction peaks of Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> annealed at 200 °C are closer to those of Bi (PDF #85-1329) than those of Sb (PDF #35-0732) because of more Bi component in the alloy. The composition and chemical state of Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> are further investigated by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b–d). The core-level XPS spectrum of Bi 4f shows two sets of doublets (4f<sub>5/2</sub> and 4f<sub>7/2</sub>). The doublets located at 162.2 and 156.9 eV correspond to Bi, while the doublets at 164.7 and 159.4 eV can be assigned to Bi<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> The presence of oxides is a direct result of metallic Bi being exposed to air. In contrast, the spectrum of Sb 3d only contains peaks assigned to Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>. This is because Sb is more active than Bi and will be preferentially oxidized, as Sb<sup>3+</sup>/Sb has a lower standard electrode potential (0.21 V <i>vs</i> SHE) than Bi<sup>3+</sup>/Bi (0.32 V <i>vs</i> SHE).</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c04366/asset/images/medium/nn0c04366_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c04366/asset/images/large/nn0c04366_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Structural characterization of Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> pyramid arrays. (a) XRD patterns reveal the formation of the Bi–Sb alloy, which shares the same structure as Bi and Sb. Peak shift is due to the size difference between Bi and Sb atoms. (b) XPS survey spectrum of Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub>. Core-level XPS spectra of (c) Bi 4f and (d) Sb 3d.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c04366/asset/images/large/nn0c04366_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.0c04366&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To demonstrate the potentiality of Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> in sodium batteries, we evaluate its electrochemical capability in half-cells. The durian-like array electrode could capitalize on the specific architecture advantages such as large accessible area, abundant active sites for electrochemical reaction, and short electron and ion transport length. As the Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> arrays can be directly adapted as binder-free battery electrodes, the conventional electrode processing is also waived. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> The sodium storage performance of Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> was assessed by cyclic voltammetry (CV) and galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD), and the results are presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a and b. The cathodic peaks located at 0.69 and 0.46 V represent the stepwise alloying of Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> with Na to Na(Bi, Sb) and to Na<sub>3</sub>(Bi, Sb), and the peak at 0.23 V might be associated with the formation of a solid–electrolyte interphase. Accordingly, two prominent peaks at 0.66 and 0.79 V in the anodic scan reflect the dealloying process of Na<sub>3</sub>(Bi, Sb) → Na(Bi, Sb) → (Bi, Sb). These redox pairs are highly reproducible in the subsequent cycles, signifying a notable reversibility. In the GCD curves, the Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> electrode displays voltage plateaus well matching the redox peaks in the CV curves. The reversible (desodiation) capacity of 449 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> is almost identical to the theoretical value (454 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) and twice that of popular insertion-type anodes. The Coulombic efficiency is 86% in the first cycle and more than 98% in the following cycles.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c04366/asset/images/medium/nn0c04366_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c04366/asset/images/large/nn0c04366_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Electrochemical performance of Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> array electrodes for sodium storage. (a) CV and (b) GCD curves upon the initial three cycles. (c) Comparison of cycling performance of Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> with Bi<sub>0.2</sub>Sb<sub>0.8</sub> and Bi<sub>0.58</sub>Sb<sub>0.42</sub> alloy arrays for 200 cycles. (d) Long-term electrode stability for 2000 cycles. Prior to testing, the electrode was precycled at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> for activation.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c04366/asset/images/large/nn0c04366_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.0c04366&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">It is worth noting that we have to trade off between capacity and the cyclability of array electrodes, as the electrode durability is highly composition-dependent. This is quite rational considering that the capacity and the volume expansion are proportional to the Sb content in the alloy. Increasing the ratio of Sb results in a more severe electrode swelling and thereby a greater possibility of electrode failure. In this regard, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c compares the cycling stability of Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> with two other pyramid-architectured electrodes with a higher Sb content. Clearly, Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> features a more robust electrochemical stability. After initial activation, the Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> electrode affords a reversible capacity of 384 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> in the first cycle and 334 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> in the 200th cycle at a rate of 0.25 A g<sup>–1</sup>. In contrast, Bi<sub>0.2</sub>Sb<sub>0.8</sub> and Bi<sub>0.58</sub>Sb<sub>0.42</sub> exhibit a much inferior durability, although they possess a higher theoretical capacity (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c04366/suppl_file/nn0c04366_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>). For instance, the Bi<sub>0.2</sub>Sb<sub>0.8</sub> electrode only retains 158 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> in the 200th cycles.</div><div class="NLM_p">More importantly, the Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> electrode features an ultralong durability at a rate of 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d reveals a capacity retention of 90% over 1000 cycles and 87% over 2000 cycles. The capacity at the 2000th cycle is 284 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and the Coulomb efficiency is ∼100%. Minimal polarization evolution occurs upon the long-term cycling, as confirmed by the GCD curves under various cycling stages shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c04366/suppl_file/nn0c04366_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>. To illustrate the notable stability of Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> electrodes, we compare the electrochemical cycling of Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> with recently reported high-performance Sb- and Bi-based electrodes in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c04366/suppl_file/nn0c04366_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>. The Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> electrode outperforms most reported anodes, strongly supporting the rationale of our bioinspired materials design.</div><div class="NLM_p">In addition to the dynamic durability, Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> exhibits a robust rate capability, as indicated by high-rate GCD and CV curves shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>. Rate cycling results reflect an outstanding reaction activity and reversibility of Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> electrodes. At rates of 0.25, 0.5, 1, and 2.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> affords reversible capacities of 362, 345, 339, and 335 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. When the rate increases by 10-fold, there is no evident distortion of voltage plateaus, and the rate capacity retention is as high as 92.5%. Moreover, the high-rate tests have little effect on the robustness of Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub>; comparable capacity delivery remains upon a second round cycling test. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c shows the CV curves at sweep rates ranging from 0.2 to 2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. At all sweep rates, Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> displays a similar position and profile of redox peaks, reflecting a robust reversibility. To quantitatively assess the kinetic robustness, we plot the CV peak current (<i>i</i><sub>p</sub>) <i>versus</i> the sweep rate (<i>v</i>) in the logarithmic format in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d. According to <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="1">1</a>,<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c04366/asset/images/medium/nn0c04366_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i3"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span>where <i>b</i> and <i>a</i> are two variables, we can distinguish the capacitive contribution from the battery contribution in the capacity. A <i>b</i> value of 1 represents a capacitance (surface) process, while a <i>b</i> value of 0.5 indicates a diffusion-controlled battery process. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> The linear fitting of log(<i>i</i><sub>p</sub>) against log(<i>v</i>) gives <i>b</i> values of 0.61 and 0.66, respectively, for the cathodic and anodic processes, suggesting a pseudocapacitive sodium storage process. This pseudocapacitive feature endows Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> with a vigorous rate performance, which is desirable for practical deployment.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c04366/asset/images/medium/nn0c04366_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c04366/asset/images/large/nn0c04366_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Kinetic analysis of Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> array electrodes upon sodium storage. (a) GCD curves measured at various current rates. (b) Rate cycling capability. (c) CV curves at varied scan rates. (d) Plots of CV peak current (<i>I</i><sub>p</sub>) as a function of scan rate (<i>v</i>).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c04366/asset/images/large/nn0c04366_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.0c04366&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Such a robust performance is closely correlated to the durian-like structure of Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub>. Besides several advantages inherited from the 3D array, the pyramid shape would play a principal role in the Na<sup>+</sup> ion reaction. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> Previous work has proposed a tip effect, which suggests a spontaneous establishment of localized electric field and high ion concentration around a sharp tip. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36 ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36 ref37" aria-label="reference 36 and 37">(36,37)</a> In this case, Na<sup>+</sup> ions would be enriched around the pyramid tips, which promotes the charge-transfer reaction of Na<sup>+</sup> ions under high current rates. To further illustrate this point, we establish an ion concentration distribution model on the electrode surface based on the Gouy–Chapman–Stern theory. This theory describes the surface charge of electrode and electrolyte solution and the distribution of charged ions in the solution. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> The theory holds that a tight Helmholtz layer and the diffusion layer will be formed at the interface between the metal electrode and the solution. The simulation results derived from the finite-element method are presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>. The finite-element method is a numerical technique for finding approximate solutions to boundary value problems for partial differential equations. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a shows the electric field distribution around the electrode tip whose radii are 5, 10, 50, and 100 nm, respectively. Significantly, the electric field intensity decreases monotonically with the increase of the tip radius and the distance away from the electrode surface. The quantitative variation of electric field intensity as a function of the electrode tip size further confirms such a trend. This local electric field would attract more Na<sup>+</sup> ions to the electrode surrounding, leading to a significant ion enrichment around the electrode tip area. This enrichment could efficiently promote the electrode reaction and avoid the depletion of Na<sup>+</sup> ions under high current rates.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c04366/asset/images/medium/nn0c04366_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c04366/asset/images/large/nn0c04366_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Finite-element analysis of distribution of electric field and Na<sup>+</sup> ion concentration around an electrode tip. (a) Schematic illustrating the distribution of electric field intensity around the electrodes with varied radii. (b) The electric field intensity as a function of the electrode radius. (c) Schematic showing the distribution of Na<sup>+</sup> concentration around the electrode.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c04366/asset/images/large/nn0c04366_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.0c04366&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To unravel the robust performance of Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub>, we monitor the morphological evolution of Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> by SEM imaging. SEM images in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c04366/suppl_file/nn0c04366_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a> confirm the basic perseverance of pyramid-shaped Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> arrays upon cycling and thus the continuous action of the tip effect. More interestingly, numerous nanopores have been developed inside Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> pyramids as a result of repeated electrode swelling and shrinking. On one hand, the formation of a porous structure results in capacity fluctuation upon initial cycles, as observed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b. On the other hand, it would allow the free penetration of electrolyte and the relaxation of electrode stress and strain, which is a great advantage for long-term Na cycling. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> This is in contrast to the Sb electrode, which gradually agglomerates and ruptures during the cycles.</div><div class="NLM_p">The mechanism of Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> with sodium was surveyed by <i>ex situ</i> XRD. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a> presents the XRD patterns of Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> at different sodiation/desodiation stages in the initial cycle. Most importantly, the characteristic peaks at 27.3° (012), 38.2° (104), and 39.8° (110) of hexagonal Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> gradually shrink until they vanish during the course of sodiation. A new peak emerging at ∼32° could be assigned to Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>(Bi,Sb). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> The presence of Na in the sodiated sample can be identified by EDS in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c04366/suppl_file/nn0c04366_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>. At the end of sodiation where the characteristic hexagonal peaks vanish, new diffraction peaks corresponding to the Na<sub>3</sub>(Bi, Sb) phase appear at 33.3° and 34.4°, confirming the Na alloying reaction. The XRD evolution in combination with electrochemical results suggest a gradually alloying process for Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub>: Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> → Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> → Na<sub>3</sub>Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub>. During the desodiation, the diffraction peaks associated with Na<sub>3</sub>Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> disappear. Simultaneously, the characteristic peaks of hexagonal Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> appear again, implying a notable structural reversibility. As we have not observed the segregation of Na<sub>3</sub>Sb and Bi<sub>3</sub>Na phase, the sodiation/desodiation proceeds possibly through a single alloy phase rather than individual components of Bi and Sb. This is reasonable, as Bi and Sb show comparable physiochemical and electrochemical properties.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c04366/asset/images/medium/nn0c04366_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c04366/asset/images/large/nn0c04366_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. Electrochemical reaction mechanism investigated by e<i>x situ</i> XRD. (a) GCD curve of the Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> electrode in the initial cycle. The capitalized letters indicate the stage for the collection of XRD patterns. (b) Evolution of XRD patterns upon the initial (de)sodiation processes.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c04366/asset/images/large/nn0c04366_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.0c04366&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">Finally, to evaluate the feasibility of Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> electrodes in practice, full cells were assembled by pairing a Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> anode with a Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C cathode. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c04366/suppl_file/nn0c04366_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a> exhibits the assembly and electrochemical performance of full cells. The full cell exhibits a discharge capacity of 109 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (based on the cathode mass) at a working voltage of 2.7 V and a capacity retention of 76% over 100 cycles. The specific energy of the Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub>//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C cell based on both active electrodes is calculated to be 203 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup>, which is greater than that of many reported sodium-ion cells. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47 ref48 ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47 ref48 ref49" aria-label="reference 47 to 49">(47−49)</a> Given optimization, the performance of the full cell could be substantially improved. Therefore, this is a clear indication that the Bi–Sb alloy array can serve as robust binder-free electrodes for sodium storage.</div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i4">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">To conclude, self-supported 3D durian-like Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> pyramid arrays have been designed and built through a template-free electrodeposition. The 3D Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> array can be used as a binder-free Na electrode, profiting from the combination of binary composition, ordered structure, and tip effect associated with the pyramid shape. The Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> array electrode exhibits a robust rate capability and cycling stability toward sodium storage, affording a reversible capability of 335 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at a high rate of 2.5 A g<sup>–1</sup> and 87% of the capacity over 2000 cycles, outperforming most recent reported alloy anodes of Sb and Bi. In addition, the full cell by pairing Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C delivers a discharge capacity of 109 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and a specific energy of 203 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup>. The promising results will encourage extensive investigations on the design of advanced anode materials for lithium-beyond battery systems. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50 ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50 ref51" aria-label="reference 50 and 51">(50,51)</a></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i5">Methods</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec4_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Synthesis of Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> Pyramid Array</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> pyramid array was grown on Cu substrates through a template-free electrodeposition. Briefly, a Cu foil (0.1 mm, 99%) was ultrasonically cleaned in CH<sub>3</sub>COOH (1 M), ethanol, and deionized water, respectively. The clean Cu foil was then added into electrode holder as the working electrode and immersed into an electrolyte consisting of deionized water (20 mL), ethanol (30 mL), ethylene glycol (50 mL), HCl (3 mL), BiCl<sub>3</sub> (0.04 M), SbCl<sub>3</sub> (0.05 M), boracic acid (0.05 M), and ethylenediamine hydrochloride (0.28 M). Electrodeposition was carried out in a three-electrode setup with Ag/AgCl as the reference electrode and platinum gauze as the counter electrode. The durian-like Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> array was galvanostatically deposited onto the Cu foil at a current of 5.7 mA cm<sup>–2</sup> for a duration of 1100 s. Alloy arrays with other compositions (Bi<sub>0.2</sub>Sb<sub>0.8</sub> or Bi<sub>0.58</sub>Sb<sub>0.42</sub>) have also been prepared by altering the concentration of Bi and Sb precursors in the electrolyte. After thorough washing, the resulting Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> arrays were placed in a tube furnace under a continuous flow of nitrogen. The annealing was run at a mild temperature of 200 °C for 1 h at a ramping rate of 2 °C min<sup>–1</sup>. The loading is determined by weighing the mass of the copper substrate before and after deposition. The average mass loading of the deposited array on Cu foil is about 2.1 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. The current efficiency for electrodeposition is about 52%, which is calculated on the basis of Faraday’s law of electrolysis.</div></div><div id="sec4_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Materials Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The array samples were characterized by XRD (Rigaku D/MAX-2000PC), SEM (Hitachi SU-8010), TEM (FEI Tecnai G2 F20), and XPS (Thermo Fisher Scientific, Escalab 250Xi). The core-level binding energy was corrected with the C 1s binding energy of 284.8 eV.</div></div><div id="sec4_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8"> Finite-Element Simulations</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The finite-element method is a numerical technique for finding approximate solutions to boundary value problems for partial differential equations. Using a finite number of unknowns to approximate an infinitely unknown real system continually, it is regarded that the solution domain consists of a small number of interconnected subdomains of countless finite elements. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> The electric field and ion distribution of electrified electrodes in the electrolyte are calculated with the COMSOL software. The electric field intensity distribution in the calculation domain is calculated by using the gradient of the electric potential distribution: <i>E</i> = −∇φ, where φ is the potential distribution in the calculation domain. Then, the current density distribution around the electrode can be calculated based on the electric field intensity <i>J</i> = σ<i>E</i> + <i>J</i><sub>e</sub>, where σ is the conductivity parameter and <i>J</i><sub>e</sub> is the applied current density. To simulate the ion concentration distribution in the solution, a model based on the Gouy–Chapman–Stern theory is established. This model combines the “electrostatic” module and the “dilute matter transfer” module to solve the coupling of the charge and the electric field intensity. The Gouy–Chapman–Stern theory describes the surface charge of the electrode and electrolyte solution and the distribution of charged ions in the solution. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> A tight Helmholtz layer and the diffusion layer will be formed at the interface between the metal and the solution. The Helmholtz layer acts as a constant capacitance (<i>C</i><sub>H</sub>) in the electrochemical reaction. The diffusion layer is a variable capacitance (<i>C</i><sub>D</sub>). Among them, the size of the diffusion layer capacitance is related to the space potential distribution and the electric field intensity distribution. The Helmholtz layer capacitance and the diffusion layer capacitance play a capacitance (<i>C</i><sub>d</sub>) role in the electrochemical field. The relationship between <i>C</i><sub>d</sub> and <i>C</i><sub>H</sub>, <i>C</i><sub>D</sub> is 1/<i>C</i><sub>d</sub> = 1/<i>C</i><sub>H</sub> + 1/<i>C</i><sub>D</sub>.</div></div><div id="sec4_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9"> Electrochemical Evaluation</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> alloy array film with an area of 0.35 cm<sup>2</sup> was directly used as the working electrode. Prior to testing, one side of the film was removed through polishing. Both 2025-type half-cells and full cells of Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub>//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C were assembled in an Ar-filled glovebox (Mikrouna). In the Na half-cell, the working electrode is a Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> film, and the counter electrode is a Na foil. As for the full cell, the anode is a Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> film, and the cathode is a composite electrode consisting of 80% Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C, 10% carbon black, and 10% polyvinylidene fluoride. The electrolyte is NaPF<sub>6</sub> (1 M) dissolved in diglyme, and the separator is a Whatman glass microfiber filter. A specific precycling was conducted on the Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> anode prior to assembly of full cells to minimize the irreversible capacity loss. This treatment involves the operation of Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub>//Na half-cells at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> for five cycles. CV was measured on an Autolab electrochemical workstation. Electrochemical sodium storage tests were performed on a LAND battery test system at room temperature.</div></div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i10">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsnano.0c04366?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsnano.0c04366</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Electrochemical performance comparison; SEM images of Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub> products; atomic arrangements in hexagonal Bi and Sb; galvanostatic curves of Bi<sub>0.2</sub>Sb<sub>0.8</sub> and Bi<sub>0.58</sub>Sb<sub>0.42</sub> counterparts; characterizations of sodiated and cycled Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.</sub>25 array electrodes; electrochemical performance of Bi<sub>0.75</sub>Sb<sub>0.25</sub>//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C full cells (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c04366/suppl_file/nn0c04366_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c04366/suppl_file/nn0c04366_si_001.pdf">nn0c04366_si_001.pdf (639.87 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsnano.0c04366" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The increasing interest in energy storage for the grid can be attributed to multiple factors, including the capital costs of managing peak demands, the investments needed for grid reliability, and the integration of renewable energy sources.  Although existing energy storage is dominated by pumped hydroelec., there is the recognition that battery systems can offer a no. of high-value opportunities, provided that lower costs can be obtained.  The battery systems reviewed here include sodium-sulfur batteries that are com. available for grid applications, redox-flow batteries that offer low cost, and lithium-ion batteries whose development for com. electronics and elec. vehicles is being applied to grid storage.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrZwDymuubZm7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh4p7Sw7EVLoA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXhsVGktL%252FJ&amp;md5=5035bfda7631ad9d075e8836e61146ef</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201703137"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Delmas, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sodium and Sodium-Ion Batteries: 50 Years of Research</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A review of rechargeable batteries for portable electronic devices</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liang, Yeru; Zhao, Chen-Zi; Yuan, Hong; Chen, Yuan; Zhang, Weicai; Huang, Jia-Qi; Yu, Dingshan; Liu, Yingliang; Titirici, Maria-Magdalena; Chueh, Yu-Lun; Yu, Haijun; Zhang, Qiang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">InfoMat</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Portable electronic devices (PEDs) are promising information-exchange platforms for real-time responses.  Their performance is becoming more and more sensitive to energy consumption.  Rechargeable batteries are the primary energy source of PEDs and hold the key to guarantee their desired performance stability.  With the remarkable progress in battery technologies, multifunctional PEDs have constantly been emerging to meet the requests of our daily life conveniently.  The ongoing surge in demand for high-performance PEDs inspires the relentless pursuit of even more powerful rechargeable battery systems in turn.  In this review, we present how battery technologies contribute to the fast rise of PEDs in the last decades.  First, a comprehensive overview of historical advances in PEDs is outlined.  Next, four types of representative rechargeable batteries and their impacts on the practical development of PEDs are described comprehensively.  The development trends toward a new generation of batteries and the future research focuses are also presented.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqgtoghhfCp0rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liGQ7A_mm-hBg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXhsVKnsbnM&amp;md5=87afe7c5c7ecd491e83e778be8936d3f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1002/batt.201800057"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Werner, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Apaydin, D.                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">An Anthraquinone/Carbon Fiber Composite as Cathode Material for Rechargeable Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Werner, Daniel; Apaydin, Dogukan H.; Portenkirchner, Engelbert</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Batteries &amp; Supercaps</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">1</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">160-168</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">BSAUBU</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The transition from rare to natural abundant materials in rechargeable batteries is becoming a grand challenge in developing a resource sustainable power supply.  Since decades, scientists attempt to circumvent the lithium's resource problem by innovating alternative active metal ions.  A cost-effective alternative to lithium is to use sodium (Na) as the carrier ion in rechargeable batteries.  We present an electrode composite material comprising anthraquinone (AQ) and nanostructured carbon fibers, as a cathode material for Na-ion batteries.  These electrodes are characterized by a large surface area, ordered porous network, large pore vol. and good elec. cond.  The material is further compared to the water sol., mono-substituted anthraquinone deriv., sulfonated 9,10-anthraquinone (SAQ).  While the SAQ/carbon fiber composite demonstrates a moderate initial discharge capacity of ∼95 mAh g-1 accompanied by substantial, initial capacity fading, the AQ/carbon fiber composite shows a remarkably high discharge capacity of ∼307 mAh g-1 and exhibit reasonable cycling stability over 115 charge/discharge cycles in a Na contg. electrolyte.  This may be best explained by the well-structured intermol. π-π stacking of the thermally evapd. AQ layers and with the subjacent carbon fibers.  Since AQ and SAQ are widely-used industrial pigments, they may offer a cost-effective, abundant and environmentally benign cathode material for secondary Na-ion and Na-flow batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpUqUL4GUokCLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liGQ7A_mm-hBg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXjsV2rur0%253D&amp;md5=6f21fbf14ddb44ba5aa42c954468d12a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1002/cey2.32"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cho, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rhee, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Park, J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Among the substantial amt. of photocatalyst materials, TiO2 has been enthusiastically studied for a few decades due to its outstanding photocatalytic activity and stability.  Recently, black TiO2 consisting of approx. 2 nm of thin disorder layer around the surface showed surprisingly high solar hydrogen generation ability.  The disorder layer of TiO2 can enhance its light absorption, charge sepn., and surface reaction abilities, however exact fundamentals of photocatalytic water-splitting pathways are still ambiguous.  Herein, recent progress and investigations on exact functions of disorder layer and its application in photocatalytic CO2 redn. will be discussed.  Throughout the comprehensive studies on disorder layer of TiO2, disorder engineering on photocatalyst materials will suggest the further extension of developing solar-fuel prodn. technologies.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp1jH-UYZVrMLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liGQ7A_mm-hBg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXhsVWitbvL&amp;md5=d5e2257c8ecc1d404d6692de9717fd1b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1002/cey2.9"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cui, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Nanodiamonds for Energy</span>. <i>Carbon Energy</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>1</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">13</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">18</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit8R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">8</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Materials Based on Antimony and Bismuth for Sodium Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Xinyan; Ni, Jiangfeng; Savilov, S.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) that efficiently store electricity into chem. energy have been extensively pursued because of their great potential for low-cost and large-scale stationary application such as smart grid and renewable energy.  Successful deployment of SIBs requires efficient anode materials that could store Na+ ions via a reversible way at reasonable rates.  Materials based on antimony and bismuth are capable of storing a high-concn. of Na+ ions via a reversible alloying reaction at suitable redox potentials, and thus have drawn substantial attention.  However, these electrode materials are facing significant tech. challenges, such as poor cond., multiple phase transformation, and severe vol. swelling and shrinking, which make efficient materials design a necessity.  In this review, we will give a latest overview of research progress in the design and application of electrode materials based on antimony and bismuth, and offer some value insights into their future development in sodium storage.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqVIimhTG5VTLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liGQ7A_mm-hBg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXht1yrt7zJ&amp;md5=8b6a7010a764c46897d2b7331e43f60c</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201700622"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lao, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Luo, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dou, S.                         cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Better Cycling Performances of Bulk Sb in Na-Ion Batteries Compared to Li-Ion Systems: An Unexpected Electrochemical Mechanism</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Darwiche, Ali; Marino, Cyril; Sougrati, Moulay T.; Fraisse, Bernard; Stievano, Lorenzo; Monconduit, Laure</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Pure micrometric antimony can be successfully used as neg. electrode material in Na-ion batteries, sustaining a capacity close to 600 mAh g-1 at a high rate with a Coulombic efficiency of 99 over 160 cycles, an extremely high capacity compared to any other compd. tested against both Li and Na.  The reaction mechanism with Na does not simply go through the alloying mechanism obsd. for Li where the intermediate species are those expected from the phase diagram.  In the case of Na, the intermediate phases are mostly amorphous and could not be precisely identified.  Surprisingly, it is evidenced that a competition takes place at the end of the discharge of the Sb/Na cell between the formation of the hexagonal and the cubic polymorphs of Na3Sb, the last being described in the literature as unstable at atm. pressure and only synthesized under high pressure (1-9 GPa).  In addn., fluoroethylene carbonate added to the electrolyte combined with an appropriate electrode formulation based on CM-cellulose, carbon black, and vapor ground carbon fibers seems to be determinant in the excellent performances of this material.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpfZ6rvnJyfxrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liGQ7A_mm-hBg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XhslCgsbnE&amp;md5=75362e7708fe6434655290ce0aa7cac0</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit11" data-doi="10.1039/c3ta13438e"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dai, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bao, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, Y.-G.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Wet Milled Synthesis of an Sb/MWCNT Nanocomposite for Improved Sodium Storage</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit12R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">12</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemical properties and morphological evolution of pitaya-like Sb@C microspheres as high-performance anode for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wu, Lin; Lu, Haiyan; Xiao, Lifen; Ai, Xinping; Yang, Hanxi; Cao, Yuliang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Pitaya-like Sb@C microspheres are prepd. successfully by facile aerosol spray drying synthesis.  Structural and morphol. characterizations reveal that the Sb@C microspheres have a uniform pitaya-like structure, with well crystd. Sb nanoparticles embedded homogeneously in the C matrix.  The Sb@C microsphere electrodes exhibit high Na storage capacity of 655 mA h g-1 at C/15 with excellent cyclability (93% of capacity retention over 100 cycles), as well as good rate capability.  Also, the morphol. evolution of the Sb@C microspheres is studied to account for its excellent electrochem. performance, caused by maintenance of the pitaya-like configuration during cycling.  This structural stability guarantees tight contact of Sb with C buffer, as well as uniform distribution of Sb to balance the localized mech. stress, ensuring excellent electrochem. performance.  The structural design and synthetic method reported in this work may provide an effective way to stabilize electrochem. performance of Na-storable alloy materials and therefore provide a new prospect for creation of cycle-stable alloy anodes for high capacity Na-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo57lFV_VztT7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljxVvDD0Nuqng"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Antimony nanoparticles anchored in three-dimensional carbon network as promising sodium-ion battery anode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Luo, Wen; Zhang, Pengfei; Wang, Xuanpeng; Li, Qidong; Dong, Yifan; Hua, Jingchen; Zhou, Liang; Mai, Liqiang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">304</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A novel composite with antimony (Sb) nanoparticles anchored in three-dimensional carbon network (denoted as SbNPs@3D-C) is successfully synthesized via a NaCl template-assisted self-assembly strategy, followed by freeze-drying and one-step in-situ carbonization.  The three-dimensional interconnected macroporous carbon framework can not only stabilize the architecture and buffer the vol. expansion for Sb nanoparticles, but also provide high elec. cond. for the whole electrode.  Consequently, as a sodium-ion battery anode, the SbNPs@3D-C delivers a high reversible capacity (456 mAh/g at 100 mA/g), stable cycling performance (94.3% capacity retention after 500 cycles at 100 mA/g) as well as superior rate capability (270 mAh/g at 2000 mA/g).  When compared with com. Sb particles, the SbNPs@3D-C exhibits dramatically enhanced electrochem. performance.  Free from expensive template sources and complex manipulation, this work might shed some light on the synthesis of low-cost and high-performance materials for the next "beyond lithium" battery generation.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpYqJH9BlxHCrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljxVvDD0Nuqng"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhvFWktLzO&amp;md5=3fc4643288a77027dd91444fa9926cb6</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit15" data-doi="10.1002/smll.201501313"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sb Nanoparticles Encapsulated in a Reticular Amorphous Carbon Network for Enhanced Sodium Storage</span>. <i>Small</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>11</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">5381</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">5387</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit15R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">15</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sb Nanoparticles Encapsulated in a Reticular Amorphous Carbon Network for Enhanced Sodium Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Min; Yang, Zhenzhong; Wang, Jiaqing; Li, Weihan; Gu, Lin; Yu, Yan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Small</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">11</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">40</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sb@3D RCN (Reticular Carbon Network) films were prepd. through electrostatic spray deposition.  The encapsulating nanosized Sb in reticular porous carbon network enables excellent storage properties with respect to Na.  More specifically, at 0.2 C the Sb@3D RCN film electrode delivers a reversible capacity of 630 mA h g-1, approaching 96% of its theor. capacity.  Cycle stability tests conducted at a rate of 3 C exhibit a reversible capacity of 224 mA h g-1 after 900 cycles.  This performance is attributed to the unique Sb@3D RCN: (i) the carbon matrix prevents Sb nanoparticles agglomeration upon prolonged cycling; (ii) the large void space within 3D RCN may buffer the vol. expansion, leading to the high cycling stability of the electrode material; and (iii) the reticular structure ensures fast transport of the electrolyte within the electrode.  This structure design can be further extended to build up various nanomaterials with this unique reticular core-shell structure for promising applications in catalysis, sensing, supercapacitors, and fuel cells.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrQ7O0ZLDjepLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljxVvDD0Nuqng"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit16R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">16</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">New Nanoconfined Galvanic Replacement Synthesis of Hollow Sb@C Yolk-Shell Spheres Constituting a Stable Anode for High-Rate Li/Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Jun; Yu, Litao; Wu, Chao; Wen, Yuren; Yin, Kuibo; Chiang, Fu-Kuo; Hu, Renzong; Liu, Jiangwen; Sun, Litao; Gu, Lin; Maier, Joachim; Yu, Yan; Zhu, Min</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
